Square and circular altars were\nused for household rituals, while altars, whose shapes were combinations of\nrectangles, triangles and trapeziums, were required for public worship.\nEgypt, Babylonia and Greece\n•\nEgyptians developed a number of geometric techniques and rules for calculating\nsimple areas and doing simple constructions. Babylonians and Egyptains used\ngeometry mostly for practical purposes and did very little to develop it as a systematic\nscience. The Greeks were interested in establishing the truth of the statements\nthey discovered using deductive reasoning. A Greek mathematician, Thales is\ncredited with giving the first known proof.\nEuclid’s Elements\n•\nEuclid around 300 B.C. collected all known work in the field of mathematics and\narranged it in his famous treatise called Elements. Euclid assumed certain properties,\nwhich were not to be proved. These assumptions are actually “obvious universal\ntruths”. He divided them into two types.",

A theorem is a mathematical statement whose truth\nhas been logically established.\nPresent Day Geometry\n•\nA mathematical system consists of axioms, definitions and undefined terms.\n•\nPoint, line and plane are taken as undefined terms.\n•\nA system of axioms is said to be consistent if there are no contradictions in the\naxioms and theorems that can be derived from them.\n•\nGiven two distinct points, there is a unique line passing through them.\n•\nTwo distinct lines can not have more than one point in common.\n•\nPlayfair’s Axiom (An equivalent version of Euclid’s fifth postulate).",

    "text": "(i) Pyramid is a solid figure, the base of which is a triangle or square or some other\npolygon and its side faces are equilateral triangles that converges to a point at the\ntop.\n(ii) In Vedic period, squares and circular shaped altars were used for household rituals,\nwhile altars whose shapes were combination of rectangles, triangles and trapeziums\nwere used for public worship.\n(iii) In geometry, we take a point, a line and a plane as undefined terms.\n(iv) If the area of a triangle equals the area of a rectangle and the area of the rectangle\nequals that of a square, then the area of the triangle also equals the area of the\nsquare.\n(v) Euclid’s fourth axiom says that everything equals itself.\n(vi) The Euclidean geometry is valid only for figures in the plane.\nSolution :\n(i) False. The side faces of a pyramid are triangles not necessarily equilateral triangles.\n(ii) True. The geometry of Vedic period originated with the construction of vedis and\nfireplaces for performing vedic rites. The location of the sacred fires had to be in\naccordance to the clearly laid down instructions about their shapes and area.\n(iii) True. To define a point, a line and a plane in geometry we need to define many\nother things that give a long chain of definitions without an end. For such reasons,\nmathematicians agree to leave these geometric terms undefined.\n(iv) True. Things equal to the same thing are equal.\n(v) True. It is the justification of the principle of superposition.\n(vi) True. It fails on the curved surfaces. For example on curved surfaces, the sum of\nangles of a triangle may be more than 180°.",

If they each gain weight\nby 2 kg, how will their new weights be compared ?\nSolution : Let x kg be the weight each of Ram and  Ravi. On gaining 2 kg, weight of\nRam and Ravi will be (x + 2) each.     "text": "Sample Question 1 : Read the following statement:\n“A square is a polygon made up of four line segments, out of which, length of  three line\nsegments are equal to the length of fourth one and all its angles are right angles”.\nDefine the terms used in this definition which you feel necessary. Are there any undefined\nterms in this? Can you justify that all angles and sides of a square are equal?\nSolution : The terms need to be defined are :\nPolygon :\nA simple closed figure made up of three or more line segments.\nLine segment : Part of a line with two end points.\nLine :\nUndefined term\nPoint :\nUndefined term\nAngle :\nA figure formed by two rays with a common initial point.\nRay :\nPart of a line with one end point.\nRight angle :\nAngle whose measure is 90°.\nUndefined terms used are : line, point.\nEuclid’s fourth postulate says that “all right angles are equal to one another.”\nIn a square, all angles are right angles, therefore, all angles are equal (From Euclid’s\nfourth postulate).\nThree line segments are equal to fourth line segment (Given).\nTherefore, all the four sides of a square are equal. (by Euclid’s first axiom “things\nwhich are equal to the same thing are equal to one another.”)",
